<div class="right_col col-a-2 col-right-profile-header">
<div class="header artist_info_header int-1 clearfix padding-left-none pfora-header">
<h1 class="hed-1 pfora-h1" itemprop="name" title="The Rolling Stones">The Rolling Stones</h1>
<div class="header_btns">
<div class="bookmark" data-musicid="R77757"></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="col-a-2 fxCol-cont-5 col-prop-1 clearfix padding-top-lrg col-right-profile-body no-border">
<div class="int-1">
<div class="content_right">
<div class="artist_bio_container clearfix">
<div class="artist_bio short_bio"><div class="artist_bio_inner">By the time the Rolling Stones began calling themselves the World's Greatest Rock &amp; Roll Band in the late '60s, they had already staked out an impressive claim on the title. As the self-consciously dangerous alternative to the bouncy Merseybeat of <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">the Beatles</a> in the British Invasion, the Stones had pioneered the gritty, hard-driving blues-based rock &amp; roll that came to define hard rock. With his preening machismo and latent maliciousness, <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Mick Jagger</a> became the prototypical rock frontman, tempering his macho showmanship with a detached, campy irony while <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Keith Richards</a> and Brian Jones wrote the blueprint for sinewy, interlocking rhythm guitars. Backed by the strong yet subtly swinging rhythm section of bassist <a class="hash" href="/bill-wymans-rhythm-kings">Bill Wyman</a> and drummer Charlie Watts, the Stones became the breakout band of the British blues scene, eclipsing such contemporaries as <a class="hash" href="/animals">the Animals</a> and <a class="hash" href="/them">Them</a>. Over the course of their career, the Stones never really abandoned blues, but as soon as they reached popularity in the U.K., they began experimenting musically, incorporating the British pop of contemporaries like <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">the Beatles</a>, <a class="hash" href="/kinks">Kinks</a>, and <a class="hash" href="/who">Who</a> into their sound. After a brief dalliance with psychedelia, the Stones re-emerged in the late '60s as a jaded, blues-soaked hard rock quintet. The Stones always flirted with the seedy side of rock &amp; roll, but as the hippie dream began to break apart, they exposed and reveled in the new rock culture. It wasn't without difficulty, of course. Shortly after he was fired from the group, Jones was found dead in a swimming pool, while at a 1969 free concert at Altamont, a concertgoer was brutally killed during the Stones' show. But the Stones never stopped going. For the next 30 years, they continued to record and perform, and while their records weren't always blockbusters, they were never less than the most visible band of their era -- certainly, none of their British peers continued to be as popular or productive as the Stones. And no band since has proven to have such a broad fan base or far-reaching popularity, and it is impossible to hear any of the groups that followed them without detecting some sort of influence, whether it was musical or aesthetic. <br><br>
Throughout their career, <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Mick Jagger</a> (vocals) and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Keith Richards</a> (guitar, vocals) remained at the core of the Rolling Stones. The pair initially met as children at Dartford Maypole County Primary School. They drifted apart over the next ten years, eventually making each other's acquaintance again in 1960, when they met through a mutual friend, Dick Taylor, who was attending Sidcup Art School with <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a>. At the time, <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> was studying at the London School of Economics and playing with Taylor in the blues band Little Boy Blue and the Blue Boys. Shortly afterward, <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> joined the band. Within a year, they had met Brian Jones (guitar, vocals), a Cheltenham native who had dropped out of school to play saxophone and clarinet. By the time he became a fixture on the British blues scene, Jones had already had a wild life. He ran away to Scandinavia when he was 16; by that time, he had already fathered two illegitimate children. He returned to Cheltenham after a few months, where he began playing with the Ramrods. Shortly afterward, he moved to London, where he played in <a class="hash" href="/alexis-korner">Alexis Korner</a>'s group, Blues Inc. Jones quickly decided he wanted to form his own group and advertised for members; among those he recruited was the heavyset blues pianist Ian Stewart. <br><br>
As he played with his group, Jones also moonlighted under the name Elmo Jones at the Ealing Blues Club. At the pub, he became reacquainted with Blues, Inc., which now featured drummer Charlie Watts, and, on occasion, cameos by <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a>. Jones became friends with <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a>, and they soon began playing together with Taylor and Stewart; during this time, <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Mick</a> was elevated to the status of Blues, Inc.'s lead singer. With the assistance of drummer Tony Chapman, the fledgling band recorded a demo tape. After the tape was rejected by EMI, Taylor left the band to attend the Royal College of Art; he would later form <a class="hash" href="/pretty-things">the Pretty Things</a>. Before Taylor's departure, the group named itself the Rolling Stones, borrowing the moniker from a <a class="hash" href="/waters-winter-cotton">Muddy Waters</a> song. <br><br>
The Rolling Stones gave their first performance at the Marquee Club in London on July 12, 1962. At the time, the group consisted of <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a>, <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a>, Jones, pianist Ian Stewart, drummer Mick Avory, and Dick Taylor, who had briefly returned to the fold. Weeks after the concert, Taylor left again and was replaced by <a class="hash" href="/bill-wymans-rhythm-kings">Bill Wyman</a>, formerly of the Cliftons. Avory also left the group -- he would later join <a class="hash" href="/kinks">the Kinks</a> -- and the Stones hired Tony Chapman, who proved to be unsatisfactory. After a few months of persuasion, the band recruited Charlie Watts, who had quit Blues, Inc. to work at an advertising agency once the group's schedule became too hectic. By 1963, the band's lineup had been set, and the Stones began an eight-month residency at the Crawdaddy Club, which proved to substantially increase their fan base. It also attracted the attention of Andrew Loog Oldham, who became the Stones' manager, signing them from underneath the Crawdaddy Club's Giorgio Gomelsky. Although Oldham didn't know much about music, he was gifted at promotion, and he latched upon the idea of fashioning the Stones as the bad-boy opposition to the clean-cut <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">Beatles</a>. At his insistence, the large yet meek Stewart was forced out of the group, since his appearance contrasted with the rest of the group. Stewart didn't disappear from the Stones; he became one of their key roadies and played on their albums and tours until his death in 1985. <br><br>
With Oldham's help, the Rolling Stones signed with Decca Records, and that June, they released their debut single, a cover of <a class="hash" href="/chuck-berry">Chuck Berry</a>'s "Come On." The single became a minor hit, reaching number 21, and the group supported it with appearances on festivals and package tours. At the end of the year, they released a version of <a class="hash" href="/plastic-ono-band">Lennon</a>-McCartney's "I Wanna Be Your Man" that soared into the Top 15. Early in 1964, they released a cover of <a class="hash" href="/buddy-holly-crickets">Buddy Holly</a>'s "Not Fade Away," which shot to number three. "Not Fade Away" became their first American hit, reaching number 48 that spring. By that time, the Stones were notorious in their homeland. Considerably rougher and sexier than <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">the Beatles</a>, the Stones were the subject of numerous sensationalistic articles in the British press, culminating in a story about the band urinating in public. All of these stories cemented the Stones as a dangerous, rebellious band in the minds of the public, and had the effect of beginning a manufactured rivalry between them and <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">the Beatles</a>, which helped the group rocket to popularity in the U.S. In the spring of 1964, the Stones released their eponymous debut album, which was followed by "It's All Over Now," their first U.K. number one. <br><br>
That summer, they toured America to riotous crowds, recording the Five by Five EP at Chess Records in Chicago in the midst of the tour. By the time it was over, they had another number one U.K. single with <a class="hash" href="/howlin-wolf">Howlin' Wolf</a>'s "Little Red Rooster." Although the Stones had achieved massive popularity, Oldham decided to push <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> into composing their own songs, since they -- and his publishing company -- would receive more money that away. In June of 1964, the group released their first original single, "Tell Me (You're Coming Back)," which became their first American Top 40 hit. Shortly afterward, a version of <a class="hash" href="/irma-thomas">Irma Thomas</a>' "Time Is on My Side" became their first U.S. Top Ten. It was followed by "The Last Time" in early 1965, a number one U.K. and Top Ten U.S. hit that began a virtually uninterrupted string of <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a>-<a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> hit singles. Still, it wasn't until the group released "(I Can't Get No) Satisfaction" in the summer of 1965 that they were elevated to superstars. Driven by a fuzz-guitar riff designed to replicate the sound of a horn section, "Satisfaction" signaled that <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> had come into their own as songwriters, breaking away from their blues roots and developing a signature style of big, bluesy riffs and wry, sardonic lyrics. It stayed at number one for four weeks and began a string of Top Ten singles that ran for the next two years, including such classics as "Get Off My Cloud," "19th Nervous Breakdown," "As Tears Go By," and "Have You Seen Your Mother, Baby, Standing in the Shadow?" <br><br>
By 1966, the Stones had decided to respond to <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">the Beatles</a>' increasingly complex albums with their first album of all-original material, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/aftermath">Aftermath</a>. Due to Brian Jones' increasingly exotic musical tastes, the record boasted a wide range of influences, from the sitar-drenched "Paint It, Black" to the Eastern drones of "I'm Going Home." These eclectic influences continued to blossom on <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/between-buttons">Between the Buttons</a> (1967), the most pop-oriented album the group ever made. Ironically, the album's release was bookended by two of the most notorious incidents in the band's history. Before the record was released, the Stones performed the suggestive "Let's Spend the Night Together," the B-side to the medieval ballad "Ruby Tuesday," on <i>The Ed Sullivan Show</i>, which forced <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> to alter the song's title to an incomprehensible mumble, or else face being banned. <br><br>
In February of 1967, <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> were arrested for drug possession, and within three months, Jones was arrested on the same charge. All three were given suspended jail sentences, and the group backed away from the spotlight as the summer of love kicked into gear in 1967. <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a>, along with his then-girlfriend <a class="hash" href="/marianne-faithfull">Marianne Faithfull</a>, went with <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">the Beatles</a> to meet the Maharishi Mahesh Yogi; they were also prominent in the international broadcast of <a class="hash" href="/tony-sheridan-beatles">the Beatles</a>' "All You Need Is Love." Appropriately, the Stones' next single, "Dandelion"/"We Love You," was a psychedelic pop effort, and it was followed by their response to Sgt. Pepper, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/their-satanic-majesties-request">Their Satanic Majesties Request</a>, which was greeted with lukewarm reviews.<br><br>
The Stones' infatuation with psychedelia was brief. By early 1968, they had fired Andrew Loog Oldham and hired Allen Klein as their manager. The move coincided with their return to driving rock &amp; roll, which happened to coincide with <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a>' discovery of open tunings, a move that gave the Stones their distinctively fat, powerful sound. The revitalized Stones were showcased on the malevolent single "Jumpin' Jack Flash," which climbed to number three in May 1968. Their next album, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/beggars-banquet">Beggar's Banquet</a>, was finally released in the fall, after being delayed for five months due its controversial cover art of a dirty, graffiti-laden restroom. An edgy record filled with detours into straight blues and campy country, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/beggars-banquet">Beggar's Banquet</a> was hailed as a masterpiece among the fledgling rock press. Although it was seen as a return to form, few realized that while it opened a new chapter of the Stones' history, it also was the closing of their time with Brian Jones. Throughout the recording of <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/beggars-banquet">Beggar's Banquet</a>, Jones was on the sidelines due to his deepening drug addiction and his resentment of the dominance of <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a>. Jones left the band on June 9, 1969, claiming to be suffering from artistic differences between himself and the rest of the band. On July 3, 1969 -- less than a month after his departure -- Jones was found dead in his swimming pool. The coroner ruled that it was "death by misadventure," yet his passing was the subject of countless rumors over the next two years. <br><br>
By the time of his death, the Stones had already replaced Brian Jones with <a class="hash" href="/mick-taylor">Mick Taylor</a>, a former guitarist for John Mayall's Bluesbreakers. He wasn't featured on "Honky Tonk Women," a number one single released days after Jones' funeral, and he contributed only a handful of leads on their next album, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/let-it-bleed">Let It Bleed</a>. Released in the fall of 1969, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/let-it-bleed">Let It Bleed</a> was comprised of sessions with Jones and Taylor, yet it continued the direction of <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/beggars-banquet">Beggar's Banquet</a>, signaling that a new era in the Stones' career had begun, one marked by ragged music and an increasingly wasted sensibility. Following <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a>'s filming of <i>Ned Kelly</i> in Australia during the first part of 1969, the group launched its first American tour in three years. Throughout the tour -- the first where they were billed as the World's Greatest Rock &amp; Roll Band -- the group broke attendance records, but it was given a sour note when the group staged a free concert at Altamont Speedway. On the advice of <a class="hash" href="/grateful-dead">the Grateful Dead</a>, the Stones hired Hell's Angels as security, but that plan backfired tragically. The entire show was unorganized and in shambles, yet it turned tragic when the Angels killed a young black man, Meredith Hunter, during the Stones' performance. In the wake of the public outcry, the Stones again retreated from the spotlight and dropped "Sympathy for the Devil," which some critics ignorantly claimed incited the violence, from their set. As the group entered a hiatus, they released the live <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/get-yer-ya-yas-out-live">Get Yer Ya-Ya's Out!</a> in the fall of 1970. It was their last album for Decca/London, and they formed Rolling Stones Records, which became a subsidiary of Atlantic Records. <br><br>
During 1970, <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> starred in Nicolas Roeg's cult film <i>Performance</i> and married Nicaraguan model Bianca Perez Morena de Macias, and the couple quickly entered high society. As <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> was jet-setting, <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> was slumming, hanging out with country-rock pioneer <a class="hash" href="/international-submarine-band">Gram Parsons</a>. <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Keith</a> wound up having more musical influence on 1971's <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/sticky-fingers">Sticky Fingers</a>, the first album the Stones released though their new label. Following its release, the band retreated to France on tax exile, where they shared a house and recorded a double album, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/exile-on-main-street">Exile on Main St.</a> Upon its May 1972 release, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/exile-on-main-street">Exile on Main St.</a> was widely panned, but over time it came to be considered one of the group's defining moments. <br><br>
Following <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/exile-on-main-street">Exile</a>, the Stones began to splinter in two, as <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> concentrated on being a celebrity and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> sank into drug addiction. The band remained popular throughout the '70s, but their critical support waned. <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/goats-head-soup">Goats Head Soup</a>, released in 1973, reached number one, as did 1974's <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/its-only-rock-n-roll">It's Only Rock 'n' Roll</a>, but neither record was particularly well received. Taylor left the band after <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/its-only-rock-n-roll">It's Only Rock 'n' Roll</a>, and the group recorded their next album as they auditioned new lead guitarists, including <a class="hash" href="/jeff-beck-group">Jeff Beck</a>. They finally settled on <a class="hash" href="/ronnie-wood">Ron Wood</a>, former lead guitarist for <a class="hash" href="/faces">the Faces</a> and <a class="hash" href="/rod-stewart">Rod Stewart</a>, in 1976, the same year they released <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/black-blue">Black n' Blue</a>, which only featured Wood on a handful of cuts. During the mid- and late '70s, all the Stones pursued side projects, with both <a class="hash" href="/bill-wymans-rhythm-kings">Wyman</a> and Wood releasing solo albums with regularity. <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> was arrested in Canada in 1977 with his common-law wife Anita Pallenberg for heroin possession. After his arrest, he cleaned up and was given a suspended sentence the following year. <br><br>
The band reconvened in 1978 to record Some Girls, an energetic response to punk, new wave, and disco. The record and its first single, the thumping disco-rocker "Miss You," both reached number one, and the album restored the group's image. However, the group squandered that goodwill with the follow-up, Emotional Rescue, a number one record that nevertheless received lukewarm reviews upon its 1980 release. <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/tattoo-you">Tattoo You</a>, released the following year, fared better both critically and commercially, as the singles "Start Me Up" and "Waiting on a Friend" helped the album spend nine weeks at number one. The Stones supported <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/tattoo-you">Tattoo You</a> with an extensive stadium tour captured in Hal Ashby's movie <i>Let's Spend the Night Together</i> and the 1982 live album Still Life. <br><br>
<a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/tattoo-you">Tattoo You</a> proved to be the last time the Stones completely dominated the charts and the stadiums. Although the group continued to sell out concerts in the '80s and '90s, their records didn't sell as well as previous efforts, partially because the albums suffered due to <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a>' notorious mid-'80s feud. Starting with 1983's Undercover, the duo conflicted about which way the band should go, with <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> wanting the Stones to follow contemporary trends and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> wanting them to stay true to their rock roots. As a result, Undercover was a mean-spirited, unfocused record that received relatively weak sales and mixed reviews. Released in 1986, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/dirty-work">Dirty Work</a> suffered a worse fate, since <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> was preoccupied with his fledgling solo career. Once <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> decided that the Stones would not support <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/dirty-work">Dirty Work</a> with a tour, <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> decided to make his own solo record with 1988's <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards/talk-is-cheap">Talk Is Cheap</a>. Appearing a year after <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a>'s failed second solo album, <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards/talk-is-cheap">Talk Is Cheap</a> received good reviews and went gold, prompting <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Jagger</a> and <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Richards</a> to reunite late in 1988. <br><br>
The following year, the Stones released <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/steel-wheels">Steel Wheels</a>, which was received with good reviews, but the record was overshadowed by its supporting tour, which grossed over 140 million dollars and broke many box office records. In 1991, the live album Flashpoint, which was culled from the <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/steel-wheels">Steel Wheels</a> shows, was released. Following the release of Flashpoint, <a class="hash" href="/bill-wymans-rhythm-kings">Bill Wyman</a> left the band; he published a memoir, <i>Stone Alone</i>, within a few years of leaving. The Stones didn't immediately replace <a class="hash" href="/bill-wymans-rhythm-kings">Wyman</a>, since they were all working on solo projects; this time, there was none of the animosity surrounding their mid-'80s projects. <br><br>
The group reconvened in 1994 with bassist Darryl Jones, who had previously played with <a class="hash" href="/miles-davis">Miles Davis</a> and <a class="hash" href="/sting-gil-evans">Sting</a>, to record and release the Don Was-produced <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/voodoo-lounge">Voodoo Lounge</a>. The album received the band's strongest reviews in years, and its accompanying tour was even more successful than the <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/steel-wheels">Steel Wheels</a> tour. On top of being more successful than its predecessor, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/voodoo-lounge">Voodoo Lounge</a> also won the Stones their first Grammy for Best Rock Album. Upon the completion of the <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/voodoo-lounge">Voodoo Lounge</a> tour, the Stones released the live, "unplugged" album <a class="hash" href="#">Stripped</a> in the fall of 1995. Similarly, after wrapping up their tour in support of 1997's <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/bridges-to-babylon">Bridges to Babylon</a>, the group issued yet another live set, No Security, the following year. A high-profile greatest-hits tour in 2002 was launched despite the lack of a studio album to support, and its album document, <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/live-licks-live">Live Licks</a>, appeared in 2004. A year later, the group issued <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/bigger-bang">A Bigger Bang</a>, their third effort with producer Don Was. <br><br>
In 2006, Martin Scorsese filmed two of the group's performances at New York City's Beacon Theatre. The resulting Shine a Light, which included guest appearances from <a class="hash" href="/buddy-guy">Buddy Guy</a>, <a class="hash" href="/jack-white">Jack White</a>, and <a class="hash" href="/christina-aguilera">Christina Aguilera</a>, was released in theaters in 2008. The accompanying soundtrack reached the number two spot on the U.K. charts. Following Shine a Light, the Stones turned their attention toward their legacy. For <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Keith Richards</a>, this meant delving into writing his autobiography <i>Life</i> -- the memoir was published to acclaim in the fall of 2010; it generated some controversy due to comments <a class="hash" href="/keith-richards-x-pensive-winos">Keith</a> made about <a class="hash" href="/mick-jagger">Mick</a> -- but the Stones in general spent time mining their archives, something they previously avoided. In 2010, they released a super deluxe edition of <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/exile-on-main-street">Exile on Main St.</a> that contained a bonus disc of rarities and outtakes, including a few newly finished songs like "Plundered My Soul." This was followed in 2011 by a super deluxe edition of Some Girls that also contained unheard songs and outtakes. That same year, the Stones opened up their Rolling Stones Archive, which offered official digital releases of classic live bootlegs like 1973's The Brussels Affair. All this was a prelude to their 50th anniversary in 2012, which the group celebrated with a hardcover book, a new documentary called Crossfire Hurricane and a new compilation called <a class="hash" href="/rolling-stones/grrr-deluxe-version">GRRR!</a>. The Stones also played a handful of star-studded concerts at the end of the year and in the first half of 2013, several of which featured guest spots from the long-departed <a class="hash" href="/mick-taylor">Mick Taylor</a>. These live shows culminated with a headlining spot at Glastonbury and two July 2013 concerts at Hyde Park; highlights from the Hyde Park shows were released that July and, later in the year, there was a home video/CD release of the concert called Sweet Summer Sun: Live in Hyde Park. ~ Stephen Thomas Erlewine, Rovi</br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></br></div></div>
<a class="full_bio_link" href="#">full bio</a>
</div>

<a class="track_link" href="/rolling-stones/grrr-deluxe-version/have-you-seen-your-mother-baby-standing-in-shadow" itemprop="url" title="Have You Seen Your Mother, Baby, Standing In The Shadow?">40. <span itemprop="name">Have You Seen Your Mother, Baby, Standing In The Shadow?</span></a>

<div class="track clearfix" itemprop="tracks" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/MusicRecording">
<meta content="Singles Collection: The London Years" itemprop="inAlbum"/>

The Recipient must be at least 13 years of age, and a resident of a country in which Pandora is authorized to operate the Pandora One service.</li>
		<li>To purchase a Pandora One Gift Subscription online, the Purchaser must provide valid registration information, a valid payment method, and other payment information at the time of purchase. By purchasing a Pandora One Gift Subscription, you authorize Pandora to charge the purchase price of the Pandora One Gift Subscription, and any applicable sales, telecommunication, excise or similar taxes to the payment method that you provide.</li>
		<li>The Purchaser is not entitled to a refund or credit for any reason, including, without limitation, if the Recipient fails to redeem or cancels the Pandora One Gift Subscription, except to the extent required by law. However, if the Purchaser purchases a Pandora One Gift Card at a retail store, the Pandora One Gift Card itself may be refundable prior to activation of the Pandora One Gift Subscription online if permitted by the retailer's return policy; see store for details.</li>
		<li>The Purchaser may not transfer a single Pandora One Gift Subscription to more than one recipient. A Pandora One Gift Subscription is fully transferable by the Recipient prior to activation or redemption. In the event of any such transfer by the Recipient, the individual who redeems or activates the Pandora One Gift Subscription shall be deemed the Recipient for purposes hereof.</li>
		<li>Recipients may redeem their valid Pandora One Gift Subscriptions at any time by using the activation code provided upon purchase. The code may be located at the bottom of a gift subscription email or print-out, above the terms and conditions, or on the back of a Pandora One Gift Card. Once redeemed and activated, the	code is void. The Pandora One Gift Subscription itself is good for the term that is set forth in your gift subscription email, print out or Pandora One Gift Card (the \"Gift Subscription Term\").</li>
		<li>Pandora One Gift Subscriptions may not be redeemed for cash, or combined with other promotional offers, free trial offers or incentives.</li>
		<li>Recipients must provide Pandora with valid registration information, including, but not limited, to an email address, birth year, zip code and gender, in order to redeem or activate the Pandora One Gift Subscription.</li>

At the end of the Gift Subscription Term, the Recipient's own Pandora One subscription will resume in accordance with the Pandora One Terms and his or her account settings, including automatic renewal, if elected by the Recipient.</li>
